/></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><i>K</i>s distributions for duplicated gene pairs in <i>C. chinensis</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene family analysis</h3><p>We performed comparative genomic analyses among 18 plant species and detected 81,585 families of homologous genes. A total of 17,964 gene families were identified in <i>C. chinensis</i>, among which 2164 and 14,356 gene families showed expansion and contraction, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Fig4">4</a>). A total of 2164 expanded gene families were annotated to KEGG pathways (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM7">S9</a>) and GO terms (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM8">S10</a>). KEGG analysis showed that most of the expanded genes were clustered in the categories of energy metabolism, signal transduction and aging. GO analysis showed that the expanded orthogroups were related to metabolic process and cellular component categories and the stimulus response, cysteine synthase, phototropism and sugar carrier terms. A total of 14,356 of the contracted gene families were involved in carbohydrate metabolism, signal transduction, secondary metabolism biosynthesis, amino acid metabolism, the nervous system, the metabolism of terpenoids and polyketides, the immune system, environmental adaptation and aging (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM9">S11</a>). The GO terms of the contracted genes were related to the cellular component, flavonoid biosynthetic process, developmental process, stimulus response, catalytic activity, biological regulation, transcription regulator activity, immune system process, signaling, growth and reproduction process categories (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM10">S12</a>). When we sought to investigate the environmental adaptation of <i>C. chinensis</i>, we found that a series of photosynthesis and energy metabolism-associated gene families exhibit significant expansion, including the NADPHâoxidase (NDHF, NDHB1, GPSA), NADH dehydrogenase (NAD1, NDHD, NAD4L, NAD7, NDHG), chlorophyll a-b binding protein (LHCB) and cytochrome P450 (CYP86) families (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM11">S13</a>). Notably, CYP450 genes are involved in the regulation of basic developmental processes such as cell differentiation and growth, indicating effects on the expression of developmental genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Fangmeng, D. et al. Overexpression of socyp85a1, a spinach cytochrome p450 gene in transgenic tobacco enhances root development and drought stress tolerance. Front. Plant. Sci. 8, 1909 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1277">19</a></sup>. Additionally, approximately half of the encoded proteins catalyze specific biochemical reactions in various metabolic pathways. To obtain insights into the evolutionary relationships among <i>C. chinensis</i> CYP450 family proteins, we constructed a phylogenetic trees based on the sequences of full-length CYP450 proteins from 216 amino acid sequences of <i>C. chinensis</i> (121) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (95) using the neighbor-joining method. The numbers of most CYP450 types, including CYP71, CYP86 and CYP90, were higher than in <i>A. thaliana</i> indicating a close relationship of the development and metabolism of <i>C. chinensis</i> plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S4</a>).</p><p>The comparison of <i>C. chinensis</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>P. alba</i>, <i>M. esculenta</i>, <i>P. euphratica</i>, <i>J. curca</i>s, <i>R. communis</i>, <i>C. pepo</i> and <i>H. brasiliensis</i> revealed that 4858 (31.37%) of the 15,485 <i>C. chinensis</i> gene families were shared by the other nine species, whereas 5,636 gene families were unique to <i>C. chinensis</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Fig6">6</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S5</a>). These 5,636 unique families have been specific to <i>C. chinensis</i> during its long history of evolution. Some of them may have been lost in other species, although we believe that some gene families originated de novo in Podostemaceae. Functional analysis performed via GO analysis revealed that these 5,636 unique families were enriched in suberin biosynthesis, cationic antimicrobial peptides, folate biosynthesis and ABC transporters (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM3">S14</a>). KEGG analysis showed that most of the 5636 unique families were clustered in the signal transduction, lipid metabolism, energy metabolism and environmental adaptation categories (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM4">S15</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="UpSet plot of the intersection of gene families in C. chinensis, A. thaliana, O. sativa, P. alba, M. esculenta, P. euphratica, J. curcas, R. communis, C. pepo and H. brasiliensis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: UpSet plot of the intersection of gene families in <i>C. chinensis, A. thaliana, O. sativa, P. alba, M. esculenta, P. euphratica, J. curcas, R. communis, C. pepo and H. brasiliensis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0269-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_269_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0269-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_269_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="511" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>The numbers of gene families (clusters) are indicated for each species and species intersection</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Phylogenetic analysis of the WRKY, NAC and resistance gene families</h3><p>A phylogenetic tree was constructed based on the WRKY gene family members from <i>A. thaliana</i> (72), <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (102) and <i>C. chinensis</i> (68) to examine the evolutionary relationships among them. According to our results, a total of 68 WRKY genes were identified in the <i>C. chinensis</i> genome and classified into 7 groups: I, IIa, IIb, IIc, IId, IIe and III. The subfamily III group was one of the most ancient WRKY types, while subfamily I had fewer members, with no more than six members in each species. The identified WRKY genes of <i>C. chinensis</i> were only classified into the IIc subfamily (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Fig7">7a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="The evolutionary tree of WRKY and NAC gene families in C. chinensis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: The evolutionary tree of WRKY and NAC gene families in <i>C. chinensis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0269-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_269_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0269-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_269_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="348" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>a</b> The evolutionary tree and expression values of WRKY box genes in <i>C. chinensis</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>. <b>b</b> The evolutionary tree and expression values of NAC box genes in <i>C. chinensis</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>. Genes in the WRKY and NAC families were clustered separately using MEGA7 software via the neighbor-joining method</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To obtain insights into the evolutionary relationships among <i>C. chinensis</i> NAC family proteins, phylogenetic trees were constructed based on the sequences of full-length NAC-box proteins from 350 amino acid sequences of <i>C. chinensis</i> (81), <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (165) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (104) using the neighbor-joining method. The 81 NAC genes of <i>C. chinensis</i> were classified into 18 categories, including NAC1, NAP, NAM and TIP subfamily genes. The number of NAM and NAP subfamily genes from the NAC family was higher than in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. We speculate that the members of the NAM and NAP subfamilies could play roles in the development and defense responses of <i>C. chinensis</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Fig7">7b</a>).</p><p>Furthermore, we analyzed the evolutionary relationships among resistance (R) gene proteins from <i>C. chinensis</i> (26) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (423) via phylogenetic tree analysis. The results showed that the R gene proteins could be classified into three groups, including TNL, CNL and RLP/RLK subfamily genes. Interestingly, the number of R gene proteins from <i>A. thaliana</i> (423) was 16.26-fold higher than that in <i>C. chinensis</i> (26), and the RLP/RLK subfamily genes of <i>C. chinensis</i> were identified and clustered. It is speculated that the presence of most R gene proteins reduces resistance and environmental adaptability, resulting in the survival of <i>C. chinensis</i> only in good-quality water (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Positively selected genes in <i>C. chinensis</i>
</h3><p>The <i>K</i>a<i>/K</i>s ratios of the single-copy genes were evaluated, and positively selected genes were identified in <i>C. chinensis</i>. A total of 490 candidate genes in <i>C. chinensis</i> underwent positive selection (<i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05). Most of which were enriched in GO terms related to the biological regulation, cellular process, developmental process, growth, metabolic process, stimulus response, catalytic activity, binding, positive regulation of biological process and cell part categories (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM12">S16</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S7</a>). KEGG enrichment analyses showed that the positively selected genes were enriched in the categories of environmental adaptation, endocrine system, metabolism of terpenoids and polyketides, lipid metabolism, energy metabolism, carbohydrate metabolism, biosynthesis of other secondary metabolites, amino acid metabolism, translation, signal transduction and cell growth (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM13">S17</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Comparative analysis of gene expression</h3><p>To obtain an overview of the transcriptome profile, principal component analysis (PCA) was performed according to normalized log10 (FPKMâ+â1) values. The first principal component (PC1), which explained 99.66% of the total variance, showed clearly different gene expression profiles between the root group and the shoot group (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S9</a>). We identified the specific genes responsible for shoot development and defense responses in <i>C. chinensis</i>. In comparison with the root group, we identified 3376 DEGs (log2 FCââ¥â2 and <i>p</i>-value â¤ 0.05) in the shoot group, including 2002 downregulated and 1374 upregulated genes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM14">S18</a>). Some of these DEGs were associated with shoot development, including WUS, ASL and STM. Among these genes, CcWUS10, CcASL4, CcASL9, CcSTM3 and CcSTM4 were expressed at low levels in the shoots, whereas CcWUS1, CcASL1, CcASL2 and CcASL7 were highly expressed in the shoots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Fig8">8a</a>, Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM14">S18</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM17">S21</a>). Podostemaceae species have extraordinary body plans showing unusual shoot organogenesis involving the modification of the SAM, making <i>C. chinensis</i> an ideal system for studying the development of roots and shoots in plants. To identify the potential key genes or transcription factors involved in shoot formation in <i>C. chinensis</i>, we identified 609 genes that were highly expressed in shoots (log2 FCââ¥â1.5 and <i>p</i>-value â¤ 0.01) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM18">S22</a>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S10</a>), and these highly expressed genes were significantly enriched in the metabolic process, stimulus response, developmental process, environmental adaptation, signal transduction, energy metabolism, carbohydrate metabolism and secondary metabolite synthesis were identified according to KEGG and GO enrichment analyses (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S12</a>; Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM19">S23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM20">S24</a>). Additionally, we found that the 609 highly expressed genes in the shoots included hormone biosynthesis and signaling genes, including 11 auxin, 1 cytokinin, 2 abscisic acid, 5 gibberellin and 1 brassinosteroid gene. The development of the plant shoot is dependent on the SAM, MYB and bHLH transcription factors, which could regulate meristem initiation, meristem function and leaf patterning. Notably, 107 transcription factors were identified from the 609 highly expressed genes in the shoots, including 11 MYB, 11 bHLH and HD-ZIP family proteins (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM1">S25</a>). In addition, we observed that the CcASL1, CcASL2, CcASL7, CcASL8 and CcSTM1 genes were involved in shoot development and were significantly expressed in specific shoot tissues (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S13</a>). Furthermore, we identified 132 and 146 MYB proteins in <i>C. chinensis</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>. These MYB genes are divided into 13 subfamilies, and the numbers of genes in subfamilies I, III, and X are greater than those in the other subfamilies of <i>A. thaliana</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Fig8">8b</a>). MYB transcription factors are widely involved in the regulation of various physiological responses and phenylpropanoid metabolic pathways, indicating a close relationship with the growth of <i>C. chinensis</i> on submerged rock surfaces in rapids and waterfalls during the rainy season.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Expression patterns of the root- and shoot-specific expression and evolutionary tree of MYB gene family in C. chinensis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8: Expression patterns of the root- and shoot-specific expression and evolutionary tree of MYB gene family in <i>C. chinensis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0269-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_269_Fig8_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0269-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_269_Fig8_HTML.png" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="383" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p><b>a</b> Heatmap showing the root- and shoot-specific expression of the members of WUS, STM, ASL and NAC. Each box represents an individual gene, and the red and blue boxes represent relatively high levels and low levels of gene expression, respectively. <b>b</b> Evolutionary tree and expression values of MYB box genes in <i>C. chinensis</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>. Genes in the MYB family were separately clustered using MEGA7 software via the neighbor-joining method. The genes of <i>C. chinensis</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> are indicated by blue and purple, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The expression of WUS, STM and ASL is involved in the initiation and maintenance of shoot development in Tristichoideae and Podostemoideae, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Katayama, N., Koi, S. &amp; Kato, M. Expression of shoot meristemless, wuschel, and asymmetric leaves1 homologs in the shoots of Podostemaceae: implications for the evolution of novel shoot organogenesis. Plant Cell 22, 2131â2140 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1643">8</a></sup>. To confirm the accuracy of the obtained unigene expression levels, nine target genes (CcWUS1, CcWUS10, CcASL1, CcASL2, CcASL4, CcASL7, CcASL9, CcSTM3, CcSTM4) associated with shoot development were analyzed in the present study (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S13</a>). The relative levels of the amplified mRNAs were evaluated according to the 2<sup>âÎÎCt</sup> method. Compared with the FPKM values, the relative expression levels of all nine unigenes determined by qRT-PCR were consistent with the RNA-seq data.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>The Podostemaceae family comprises ~300 species classified into ~50 genera, among which 3 genera, <i>Cladopus</i>, <i>Terniopsis</i> and <i>Hydrobryum</i>, have been confirmed from Fujian, Hainan, Guangdong and Yunnan in China (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S14</a>). Recently, a set of studies assessing the phylogeny, classification, biogeography and morphological evolution of the Podostemaceae family were reported. Katayama et al. examined the expression patterns of the STM, WUS and ARP genes during shoot development in Tristichoideae and Podostemoideae and tried to reveal the genetic basis for the evolution of shoots in Podostemaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Katayama, N., Koi, S. &amp; Kato, M. Expression of shoot meristemless, wuschel, and asymmetric leaves1 homologs in the shoots of Podostemaceae: implications for the evolution of novel shoot organogenesis. Plant Cell 22, 2131â2140 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1673">8</a></sup>. Mutations in the WUS gene of <i>A. thaliana</i> reported by Hamada et al. halted the entire process of SAM formation and resulted in the production of no juvenile leaves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hamada, S. et al. Mutations in the WUSCHEL gene of Arabidopsis thaliana result in the development of shoots without juvenile leaves. Plant J. 24, 91â101 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1681">13</a></sup>. Here, we assembled the genome of <i>C. chinensis</i> to the superscaffold scale using the Hi-C technique. The <i>C. chinensis</i> genome assembly has been vastly improved by Hi-C analysis, producing an N50 scaffold size for the assembly of 21.22âMb, compared to the N50 contig size of 1.42âMb for the de novo-assembled genome obtained with Canu software. These results demonstrated that a high-quality <i>C. chinensis</i> genome was produced and suggested that the numbers of repetitive sequences and ncRNA sequences were relatively high in <i>C. chinensis</i> compared with other dicotyledon species. The present genome provides a good reference for understanding the distinct aerial shoot and underground root system traits of <i>C. chinensis</i> and related species.</p><p>According to the NCBI taxonomy database, <i>C. chinensis</i> is classified into Podostemonaceae of Malpighiales based on morphological characteristics and partial nucleotide alignment (<i>rbc</i> L, <i>atp</i>B, 18âS rRNA), which is consistent with the integrated system of the angiosperm phylogeny group (APG) classification (APG, 1998; APG II, 2003; APG III, 2009; APG IV, 2016)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Chase, M. W. et al. An update of the angiosperm phylogeny group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1â20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1713">20</a></sup>. However, Podostemonaceae was removed from Malpighiales and classified into Podestemales in the integrated system of the classification of flowering plants by Cronquist<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Cronquist, A. Angiosperm orders and families. (Book Reviews: An Integrated System of Classification of Flowering Plants). Science 216, 1217â1218 (1981)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1717">21</a></sup> and Wu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wu, Z. Y. et al. (eds) The families and genera of angiosperms in China (Science Press, 2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1722">22</a></sup>. Based on a concatenated sequence alignment of <i>C. chinensis</i> and 17 other plant species, a phylogenetic tree was reconstructed (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Fig4">4a</a>). The bootstrap values were &gt;99% for all the relationships. <i>P. alba</i>, <i>J. curcas</i>, <i>M. esculenta</i>, <i>R. communis</i>, <i>P. euphratica</i>, <i>H. brasiliensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> were grouped together in Malpighiales<i>. C. chinensis</i> phylogenetically diverged into an independent branch approximately 106 million years ago (Mya), after the divergence of the Brassicales lineage 108 Mya but before the divergence of Malpighiales (82 Mya) and <i>Rosales</i> (102 Mya). We found that the internal branches separating Brassicales, Podestemales, Rosales, and Malpighiales were very short, as shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Fig4">4a</a>, suggesting the rapid diversification of these major clades. We performed coalescent analysis with RAxML and ASTRAL to estimate the species tree from gene trees. This result showed that <i>C. chinensis</i> phylogenetically clustered into Malpighiales, which is inconsistent with the findings of Cronquist<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Cronquist, A. Angiosperm orders and families. (Book Reviews: An Integrated System of Classification of Flowering Plants). Science 216, 1217â1218 (1981)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1767">21</a></sup> and Wu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wu, Z. Y. et al. (eds) The families and genera of angiosperms in China (Science Press, 2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1771">22</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Fig4">4b</a>). Based on our data, we speculated that <i>C. chinensis</i> may belong to the order Malpighiales, which is consistent with the conclusions from the APG and NCBI Taxonomy.</p><p>KEGG and GO analyses showed that the cytochrome P450 (CYP) gene and serine/threonine protein kinase families were significantly contracted in the <i>C. chinensis</i> genome (Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM8">S10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM9">S11</a>). The cytochrome P450 gene family (mostly the CYP81, CYP90, CYP85, CYP4 and CYP7 families) is related to isoflavonoid metabolic processes that regulate secondary metabolite synthesis and affect broad environmental adaptability. Previous research suggests that the cytochrome P450 gene family is important for the regulation of basic developmental processes. For example, a previous study showed that high levels of CYP85 family gene expression alleviate the jasmonate response, resulting in longer primary roots and more lateral roots and enhanced drought tolerance in tobacco<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Fangmeng, D. et al. Overexpression of socyp85a1, a spinach cytochrome p450 gene in transgenic tobacco enhances root development and drought stress tolerance. Front. Plant. Sci. 8, 1909 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1793">19</a></sup>. The ROTUNDIFOLIA3 (ROT3) locus of <i>A. thaliana</i>, which is involved in the regulation of leaf morphogenesis, harbors a CYP90 sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kim, T. G. et al. Changes in the shapes of leaves and flowers upon overexpression of cytochrome P450 in Arabidopsis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 96, 9433â9437 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1801">23</a></sup>. Serine/threonine protein kinases are involved in the response to environmental stresses (high salt, drought and low temperature) and regulate multiple life processes in cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bhaskar, G. et al. Spermidine-mediated in vitro phosphorylation of transcriptional regulator osbz8 by snf1-type serine/threonine protein kinase sapk4 homolog in indica rice. Acta Physiol. Plant 34, 1321â1336 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1805">24</a></sup>. It is speculated that the contraction of these types of gene families affects the accumulation of secondary metabolites and reduces environmental adaptability, resulting in the survival of <i>C. chinensis</i> only in good-quality water.</p><p>ATP is an energy carrier and is conserved in the form of NADH, and FADH<sub>2</sub> must be converted to ATP (oxidative phosphorylation). NADH and NADPH undergo spontaneous enzymatic reactions that participate in photosynthesis induced by light via the phytochrome system and respiratory metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, J., Wang, C. X. &amp; Ying, W. H. Sirt2 and akt mediate nad+-induced and nadh-induced increases in the intracellular atp levels of bv2 microglia under basal conditions. Neuroreport 29, 65 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1817">25</a></sup>. The chlorophyll a-b-binding proteins are located in the chloroplast thylakoid membrane, where Chl a and Chl b bind to form protein complexes that transfer absorbed light energy to photosystem reactions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Krol, M. Chlorophyll a/b-binding proteins, pigment conversions, and early light- induced proteins in a chlorophyll b-less barley mutant. Plant Physiol. 107, 873â883 (1995)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1821">26</a></sup>. KEGG and GO analysis showed that most of the expanded genes in the <i>C. chinensis</i> genome are involved in plant energy metabolism, especially oxidative phosphorylation (NADH and NADPH) and photosynthesis (chlorophyll a-b-binding proteins) (Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM6">S8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM7">S9</a>). Thus, it is likely that the significant expansion of energy metabolism-associated genes in <i>C. chinensis</i> could increase oxidative phosphorylation and photosynthesis, which would be beneficial for the growth of the plants on submerged rock surfaces and for bearing flowers exposed to the air when the water level decreases.</p><p>Katayama et al. analyzed the expression patterns of key regulatory genes related to shoot development (i.e., STM, WUS and ARP orthologs) in Tristichoideae and Podostemoideae. They found that STM and WUS were expressed in the floral meristem, similar to the pattern in model plants. Because of limited genome information, it was impossible for these authors to annotate all members of the STM, WUS and ASL gene families. The probes used for in situ hybridization in their studies could only indicate the expression profiles of these genes according to the coexpression of all gene family members<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Katayama, N., Koi, S. &amp; Kato, M. Expression of shoot meristemless, wuschel, and asymmetric leaves1 homologs in the shoots of Podostemaceae: implications for the evolution of novel shoot organogenesis. Plant Cell 22, 2131â2140 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1842">8</a></sup>. Based on the available genome information and transcriptome data, we annotated all members of these gene families and analyzed their gene expression patterns. Additionally, we found that the expression levels of different members of these gene families were upregulated or downregulated to very different extents other in shoots. In this study, we performed transcriptome sequencing to identify the genes that are involved in the diversity of SAM establishment in whole shoots and roots. In future work, we will perform single-cell transcriptome analysis to evaluate the expression patterns of these genes in fine fractions of different development stages (e.g., meristem, young shoot, and mature shoot) byâlaser capture microdissection to reveal the roles of STM, WUS and ASL gene family members in the shoot development of <i>C. chinensis</i>.</p><p>The NAC proteins are one of the largest families of transcription factors in plants; these proteins regulate gene expression during the life of plants and exhibit important biological functions in response to both stress and plant growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Kim, S. Y. et al. Exploring membrane-associated nac transcription factors in arabidopsis: implications for membrane biology in genome regulation. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 203â213 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1852">27</a></sup>. We annotated 81 members of the NAC gene family in <i>C. chinensis</i>, and the numbers belonging to the NAM and NAP subfamilies were much higher than those of other subfamilies. NAC102 is an important gene of the NAM subfamily that is thought to participate in the regulation of seed germination in flooded environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Kim, S. Y. et al. Exploring membrane-associated nac transcription factors in arabidopsis: implications for membrane biology in genome regulation. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 203â213 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1859">27</a></sup>. Our transcriptome analysis showed that some genes exhibiting significant expression in shoots were members of the NAC gene family, including NAC2, NAC5, NAC8, NAC29, NAC47, NAC73, NAC83 and NAC102. We also found that among these highly expressed NAC genes, NAC29 (Cladopus_020264), NAC47 (Cladopus_004681) and NAC83 (Cladopus_004734) were included in the genes that were duplicated via a WGD event in <i>C. chinensis</i> superscaffold no. 4 and superscaffold no. 6 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S15</a>). Additionally, the gene pairs (Cladopus_020264-Cladopus_004681, Cladopus_004734-Cladopus_025202) exhibited 97.8 and 97.0% identity scores, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM5">S26</a>), suggesting that they were functionally redundant and were likely duplicated during the evolution of shoot development in <i>C. chinensis</i>. It is speculated that the presence and expression of these genes play roles in the shoot development and environmental adaptation of <i>C. chinensis</i>. However, which of these genes are beneficial to the environmental adaptation and shoot development of <i>C. chinensis</i> remains to be further studied and analyzed.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome sequencing and de novo assembly</h3><p>The root, leaf and shoot samples of <i>C. chinensis</i> (sample number CBH03085, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S16</a>) used for genome sequencing and assembly were collected from the Tingjiang River in Changting, Fujian, China (E116<sup>ã</sup>25'10", N25<sup>ã</sup>51'03", Alt. 281.3âm)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Luo, L. Q. et al. Study on the biological characteristics of Cladopus chinensis (Podostemaceae). Bull. Biol. 49, 15â16 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1904">28</a></sup>. The voucher specimens of <i>C. chinensis</i> were deposited in the Plant Herbaria of the College of Life Science, Fujian Normal University (collection number FNU0039809). To generate enough short and long reads for the genome assembly of <i>C. chinensis</i>, next-generation sequencing (NGS) on the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform and third-generation sequencing (TGS) on the PacBio SEQUEL platform were applied for genome sequencing. Genomic DNA was produced via the CTAB method, and the integrity of the DNA was checked via agarose gel electrophoresis. The purity and concentration of the DNA were analyzed by using a NanoDrop 2000 spectrophotometer (Thermo Scientific, USA). For Illumina X Ten sequencing, we constructed a paired-end library with 250 base pair (bp) sequences using the method indicated by the manufacturer. As a result, 125.95âGb (~140X coverage of the estimated genome size, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Tab1">1</a>) of short reads were generated from the Illumina platform, which were further cleaned by using Trimmomatic (version 0.36) with the default parameters, resulting in 106.2âGb (~118Ã, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Tab1">1</a>) of cleaned data for the following analysis. For PacBio library construction and sequencing, SMRTbell libraries (~20 Kbp) were obtained according to the PacBio protocol. After the removal of adapters, 95.36âGb of subreads (~105Ã, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Tab1">1</a>) were corrected, trimmed and assembled using CANU (version 1.6) with the parameters corOutCoverageâ=â80 and corMinCoverageâ=â0. To improve accuracy, the primary contigs were further filtered with the Pilon program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, 112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1924">29</a></sup> using 106.2âGb (118Ã) of Illumina paired-end reads. We summed the statistics of the assemblies in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Tab1">1</a>. Genome completeness was assessed using the BUSCO databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1932">30</a></sup> with embryophyta_odb10 models.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Estimation of genome size and heterozygosity</h3><p>Weused NGS short reads and a K-mer-based method to estimate the genome size, heterozygosity and repeat content of <i>C. chinensis</i>. Approximately 106.2âGb of reads (250âbp) were generated and used to determine the abundance of 21-K-mers in the Illumina data. The frequency of 21-K-mers was counted with Jellyfish software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Guillaume, C. et al. A model-based method for investigating bioenergetic processes in autotrophically growing eukaryotic microalgae: application to the green algae Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Biotechnol. Progre. 27, 631â640 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1947">31</a></sup>.</p><p>Root, leaf and shoot samples of <i>C. chinensis</i> were prepared according to a previously reported protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="DoleÅ¾el, J. Flow cytometric analysis of nuclear DNA content in higher plants. Phytochem. Anal. 2, 143â154 (1991)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1957">32</a></sup> and stained with propidium iodide (50âmg/ml). Quantification was performed using flow cytometry in a BD FACSCalibur cytometer (Becton Dickinson, San Jose, CA), and the results were calculated as the ratio of the mean fluorescence of <i>C. chinensis</i> to that of <i>Zea mays</i> B73. The estimated genome size of <i>C. chinensis</i> was 835âÂ±â5.52âMb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">High-quality assembly using Hi-C technology</h3><p>Samples were examined for the integrity of nuclei by DAPI staining to ensure the quality of the Hi-C library. Samples with confirmed high-quality nuclei were subjected to the Hi-C procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Chen, P. et al. The effect of environment on the microbiome associated with the roots of a native woody plant under different climate types in China. Appl. Microbiol. Biot. 12, 661â677 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1978">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yang, X. L. et al. The chromosome-level quality genome provides insights into the evolution of the biosynthesis genes for aroma compounds of Osmanthus fragrans. Hortic. Res. 5, 72 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1981">34</a></sup>. Chromatin was digested with the restriction enzyme <i>Mbo</i> I or <i>Hin</i>d III and ligated together in situ after biotinylation. DNA fragments were enriched via the interaction of biotin and blunt-end ligation and then subjected to HiSeq sequencing. From Hi-C library sequencing, approximately 96.4âGb of data were generated (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#Tab1">1</a>). The sequencing reads were mapped to the <i>C. chinensis</i> genome with Bowtie software. We applied an iterative alignment method to increase the read mapping ratio. We aligned the two read ends to the genome independently, and only the read pairs in which both ends were uniquely aligned to the reference genome were used for the detection and filtering of valid interaction products by using HiC-Pro (version 2.7.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d28073e1998">35</a></sup>. The order and direction of scaffolds/contigs were clustered into superscaffolds by using LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2002">36</a></sup> based on the relationships among valid reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">RNA extraction and sequencing</h3><p>Total RNA was prepared from <i>C. chinensis</i> roots and shoots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM2">S17</a>) using TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen, California, USA). A NanoDrop 2000 spectrophotometer (Waltham, MA, USA) and an Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies, USA) were applied to check RNA quality; the absorbance at 260ânm/280ânm was 1.8, and the RIN value was 9.1. Equal amounts of RNA from each tissue were used for cDNA library construction. Approximately 77.7âGb of transcript data were produced for <i>C. chinensis</i> from the Illumina HiSeq X Ten sequencing platform and processed using Trimmomatic (version 0.36) with the default parameters. Reads originating from RNA-seq were aligned to the reference genome using HISAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Keel, B. N. &amp; Snelling, W. M. Comparison of burrows-wheeler transform-based mapping algorithms used in high-throughput whole-genome sequencing: application to illumina data for livestock genomes. Front. Genet. 9, 35 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2023">37</a></sup>. FPKM values and read counts were estimated using Stringtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Pertea, M. et al. Stringtie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from rna-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2027">38</a></sup> and Ballgown<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of rna-seq experiments with hisat, stringtie and ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2032">39</a></sup>. The differential expression of genes was analyzed using edgeR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Nikolayeva, O. &amp; Robinson, M. D. Edger for differential rna-seq and chip-seq analysis: an application to stem cell biology. Methods Mol. Biol. 1150, 45â79 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2036">40</a></sup>, for which the criteria were a log2-fold change (FC)ââ¥â1 and a false discovery rate (FDR)ââ¤â0.05.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Genome annotation</h3><p>In the <i>C. chinensis</i> genome, de novo- and homology-based approaches were combined to search TEs and other repetitive sequences. We identified repeat sequences using Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2052">41</a></sup>, LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 265â268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2056">42</a></sup>, RepeatProteinMask and RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences (Calgary Univ. Press, 2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2060">43</a></sup> and used Tandem Repeats Finder to search for tandem repeats in the genome assembly with the following parameters: Mismatchâ=â7, Matchâ=â2, Deltaâ=â7, PIâ=â10, Minscoreâ=â50, PMâ=â100, and MaxPeridâ=â2,000. Using LTR_FINDER (version 1.0.6), a de novo repeat library was built. Subsequently, we aligned the genome sequences to Repbase TE (version 3.2.9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2064">44</a></sup> by using RepeatMasker for the searching of known repeat sequences and mapping onto the de novo repeat libraries to identify novel types of repeat sequences. tRNAscan-SE (version 2.0.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Chan, P. P. Trnascan-se on-line: integrating search and context for analysis of transfer rna genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, 54â57 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2069">45</a></sup> was used to detect reliable tRNA positions, and noncoding RNA (ncRNA) was predicted by searching the RFAM (version 12.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Gardner, P. P. et al. Rfam: updates to the rna families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 136â140 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2073">46</a></sup> databases by using Infernal software (version 1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2077">47</a></sup> with the default parameters. Centromere and telomere repeats were identified with Tandem Repeats Finder (version 4.07b)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2081">41</a></sup>. We transformed the resulting âdat_dir fileâ into a GFF3 file, which was used to identify centromeric and telomeric repeats.</p><p>The <i>C. chinensis</i> genome assembly was annotated via the following approaches: homology-based, transcriptome-based, and <i>ab initio</i> annotation. Thirteen representative species were selected to perform homology annotation, including <i>Manihot esculenta</i> (<i>M. esculenta</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Daniell, H. et al. The complete nucleotide sequence of the cassava (Manihot esculenta) chloroplast genome and the evolution of atpf in Malpighiales: rna editing and multiple losses of a group ii intron. Theor. Appl. Genet. 116, 723â737 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2100">48</a></sup>, <i>Populus euphratica</i> (<i>P. euphratica</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Gao, G., Zhang, X., Tengfei, Y. U. &amp; Liu, B. Comparison of three evapotranspiration models with eddy covariance measurements for a Populus euphratica in an arid region of northwestern China. J. Arid Land 8, 146â156 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2111">49</a></sup>, <i>Populus alba</i> (<i>P. alba</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Liu, Y. J., Wang, X. R. &amp; Zeng, Q. Y. De novo assembly of white poplar genome and genetic diversity of White poplar population in irtysh river basin in china. Sci. China 62, 3â12 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2121">50</a></sup>, <i>Jatropha curcas</i> (<i>J. curca</i>s)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kancharla, N. et al. De novo sequencing and hybrid assembly of the biofuel crop Jatropha curcas L.: identification of quantitative trait loci for geminivirus resistance. Genes 10, 1 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2132">51</a></sup>, <i>Ricinus communis</i> (<i>R. communis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep eudicot phylogeny and their temporal diversification using nuclear genes from transcriptomic and genomic datasets. New Phytol. 214, 1338â1354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2142">52</a></sup>, <i>Hevea brasiliensis</i> (<i>H. brasiliensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Tang, C. R. et al. The rubber tree genome reveals new insights into rubber production and species adaptation. Nat. Plants 23, 73â78 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2153">53</a></sup>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (<i>A. thaliana</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Zapata, L. et al. Chromosome-level assembly of Arabidopsis thaliana reveals the extent of translocation and inversion polymorphisms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 52â57 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2163">54</a></sup>, <i>Cucurbita pepo</i> (<i>C. pepo</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Monteropau, J. et al. De novo assembly of the zucchini genome reveals a whole genome duplication associated with the origin of the Cucurbita genus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1161â1171 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2174">55</a></sup>, <i>Fragaria vesca</i> (<i>F. vesca</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109â116 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2184">56</a></sup>, <i>Malus domestica</i> (<i>M. domestica</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Daccord, N. et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat. Genet. 49, 1099â1106 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2195">57</a></sup>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (<i>P. trichocarpa</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep eudicot phylogeny and their temporal diversification using nuclear genes from transcriptomic and genomic datasets. New Phytol. 214, 1338â1354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2205">52</a></sup>, <i>Pyrus x bretschneideri</i> (<i>P. bretschneideri</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wu, J. et al. The genome of the pear (Pyrus bretschneideri rehd). Genome Res. 23, 396â408 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2216">58</a></sup> and <i>Rosa chinensis</i> (<i>R. chinensis</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Raymond, O. et al. The Rosa genome provides new insights into the domestication of modern roses. Nat. Genet. 50, 772â777 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2226">59</a></sup>. The protein sequences of these species were aligned to <i>C. chinensis</i> genome sequences using TBLASTN software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Williams, T. H. Computer software design for pediatric practice. Clin. Pediatr. 23, 5â11 (1984)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2234">60</a></sup> with an E-value â¤ 1e-5. Genewise (version 2.2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Birney, E. &amp; Durbin, R. Using genewise in the drosophila annotation experiment. Genome Res. 10, 547â548 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2238">61</a></sup> was utilized to predict the exact gene structures based on all TBLASTN results. We used Cufflinks (version 2.2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and abundance estimation from RNA-Seq reveals thousands of new transcripts and switching among isoforms. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511â515 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2242">62</a></sup> to preliminarily identify gene structures. Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, 435â439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2246">63</a></sup> was used for <i>ab initio</i> annotation with the repeat-masked genome sequences. We integrated all genes predicted from the three annotation procedures with MAKER software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="None. Molecular biology: Protein maker and gene regulator. Nature 473, 127â127 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2254">64</a></sup>.</p><p>Functional annotation of the protein-coding genes was carried out by using BLASTP with an E-value â¤ 1e-5 in four integrated protein sequence databases: eggNOG, GO, COG and KEGG. We used InterProScan (version 4.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2261">65</a></sup> and HMMER (version 3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Klingenberg, H. et al. Protein signature-based estimation of metagenomic abundances including all domains of life and viruses. Bioinformatics 29, 973â980 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2265">66</a></sup> to annotate protein domains by searching the INTERPRO (version 32.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Daly, T. K., Sutherland-Smith, A. J. &amp; David, P. In silico resurrection of the major vault protein suggests it is ancestral in modern Eukaryotes. Genome Biol. Evol. 5, 1567â1583 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2269">67</a></sup> and Pfam (version 27.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Bateman, A. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 263â266 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2273">68</a></sup> databases. Gene Ontology (GO) terms were produced from the InterPro or Pfam entry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene Ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25â29 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2277">69</a></sup>. The pathways were assigned through BLAST searches in the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) database (release 53) with an E-value â¤ 1e-5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Kanehisa, M. et al. Data, information, knowledge and principle: back to metabolism in KEGG. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, 199â205 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2282">70</a></sup>. The functions of the genes were predicted and classified using the Clusters of Orthologous Groups (COG) database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Tatusov, R. L. et al. The COG database: a tool for genome-scale analysis of protein functions and evolution. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 33â36 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2286">71</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Phylogenetic analysis and divergence time estimation</h3><p>We investigated the relationships of <i>C. chinensis</i> with 17 other species and performed a phylogenetic analysis based on protein-coding genes from the <i>C. chinensis</i> genome and 17 other species. We extracted and downloaded the protein sequences of single-copy genes from 18 species from the NCBI database, including <i>M. esculenta</i>, <i>P. euphratica</i>, <i>P. alba</i>, <i>J. curca</i>s, <i>R. communis</i>, <i>H. brasiliensis</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. pepo</i>, <i>F. vesca</i>, <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>P. bretschneideri</i>, <i>R. chinensis</i>, <i>Brassica oleracea</i> (<i>B. oleracea</i>), <i>Brassica rapa</i> (<i>B. rapa</i>), <i>Raphanus sativus</i> (<i>R. sativus</i>) and <i>Oryza sativa</i> (<i>O. sativa</i>). The similarities among the proteins from all species were searched in an all-to-all manner by using BLASTP software with an E-value â¤ 1e-5. By using OrthoFinder software (version 2.27)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2371">72</a></sup>, we generated multiple sequence alignments for the protein sequences in each single-copy family with the default parameters as well as for phylogenetic tree construction. We designated <i>O. sativa</i> as the outgroup of the phylogenetic tree. The phylogenetic relationships were constructed through the superalignment of the coding DNA sequences (CDSs) using the maximum likelihood (ML) method, and the divergence time between species was estimated using the MCMCtree program of PAML (<a href="http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html">http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html</a>). The TimeTree database (<a href="http://www.time.org/">http://www.time.org/</a>) was used for the divergence time recalibration of these plant species. The CDSs were aligned with the guidance of the protein alignments and then concatenated into the superalignment matrix of each family. We compared the cluster size differences between the ancestor and each species and analyzed the expansion and contraction of the gene families by using CAFE software (version 2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Xu, Y. et al. Removal behavior research of orthophosphate by CaFe-layered double hydroxides. Desalin. Water Treat. 57, 7918â7925 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2386">73</a></sup>. For coalescent analysis, the concatenated alignment of single-copy homologous genes for 18 species was used as the input for ML inference with RAxML (version 7.2.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Stamatakis, A. Raxml version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2390">74</a></sup>. The alignment was partitioned by the genes, and ProtTest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Abascal, F. et al. Prottest: selection of best-fit models of protein evolution. Bioinformatics 21, 2104â2105 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2395">75</a></sup> was used to select the appropriate model of amino acid substitution for each partition. We used the -f a option of RAxML to generate 200 rapid bootstrap replicates, followed by a search for the best-scoring ML tree, and then used ASTRAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Chao, Z. et al. Astral-iii: polynomial time species tree reconstruction from partially resolved gene trees. BMC Bioinformatics 19, 153 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2399">76</a></sup> to estimate the species tree from the gene trees.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Synteny analysis</h3><p>The conserved paralogs of the protein sequences of <i>C. chinensis</i> were obtained with BLASTP (E-valueââ¤â1E-5). By using MCScanX (<a href="http://chibba.pgml.uga.edu/mcscab2">http://chibba.pgml.uga.edu/mcscab2</a>), we identified collinearity blocks in the genome. The Circos tool (<a href="http://www.circos.ca">http://www.circos.ca</a>) was used to map gene density, GC content and repeat content as well as gene synteny on individual pseudochromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Whole-genome duplication</h3><p>We took advantage of the high-quality genome of <i>C. chinensis</i>, analyzed WGD events and determined the source of the high number of genes in <i>C. chinensis</i>. First, the protein sequences from <i>C. chinensis</i> were searched to identify syntenic blocks by using BLASTP with an E-value â¤ 1e-5. We identified gene synteny and collinearity by using MCScanX software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, 49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2439">77</a></sup> and calculated the synonymous substitution rate (<i>K</i>s) for syntenic gene pairs using <i>K</i>a<i>K</i>s_Calculator software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Zhang, Z. et al. KaKs_calculator: calculating ka and ks through model selection and model averaging. Genom. Proteom. Bioinf. 4, 259â263 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2453">78</a></sup> and the Nei-Gojobori method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Nei, M. &amp; Gojobori, T. Simple methods for estimating the numbers of synonymous and nonsynonymous nucleotide substitutions. Mol. Biol. Evol. 3, 418â426 (1986)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2457">79</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Quantitative real-time PCR (qRT-PCR) validation</h3><p>Total RNA was extracted from the three replicates using the TransZol Up Plus RNA Kit (Transgen Biotech, Beijing). Primers were designed using Primer Premier 5.0, and the sequences are listed in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#MOESM5">S27</a>. qRT-PCR was performed using the ABI 7300 Real-time PCR System (Framingham, MA, USA) with SYBR Green PCR Master Mix (TaKaRa) following procedures described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Wang, H. Y. et al. Use of hTERT and HPV E6/E7 mRNA RT-qPCR TaqMan assays in combination for diagnosing high-grade cervical lesions and malignant tumors. Am. J. Clin. Pathol. 143, 344 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2472">80</a></sup>. All PCR assays were performed in triplicate. The reference gene was glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate dehydrogenase (GAPDH). The relative expression level was quantified via the 2<sup>âÎÎCt</sup> method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Wang, X. et al. Expression analysis of KAP9.2 and Hoxc13 genes during different cashmere growth stages by qRT-PCR method. Mol. Biol. Rep. 41, 5665â5668 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0269-5#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d28073e2478">81</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The genome sequence data that support the findings of this study have been deposited in the BIG Sub system under BioProject accession number CRA002215 (<a href="http://bigd.big.ac.cn/gsa/s/a118ohEn">http://bigd.big.ac.cn/gsa/s/a118ohEn</a>). Raw sequencing data for RNA-Seq were used for annotation and biological analyses and have been deposited in the BIG Sub system under BioProject accession number CRA002218 (<a href="http://bigd.big.ac.cn/gsa/s/0VZ87KD1">http://bigd.big.ac.cn/gsa/s/0VZ87KD1</a>).</p>
